Preface
This thesis is the completion of six months of work, carried out at the Technical University of
Denmark, in the Department of Electrical engineering, from January 2017 to July 2017. The
report describes the clinical relevance, theory, and methods used to obtain the ﬁnal results
of 2D ﬁnite element models of the human heart, as well as an explanation and discussion of
these results. The thesis is a requirement for obtaining the Master’s degree in Biomedical
Engineering from DTU, and it accounts for 35 ECTS points.
iii
==PAGE:6==
iv
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
It is believed that the understanding of alterations in the left ventricular blood ﬂow pattern
can give a better insight to cardiovascular diseases (CVDs), and be used clinically for pre-
vention, evaluation, and treatment of CVDs. In this study, 2D ﬁnite element (FE) models
for analysis of left ventricular blood ﬂow are developed by combining Computational Fluid
Dynamics (CFD), Computed Tomography (CT), and ultrasound. The models include seg-
mentation of the left ventricle from CT scans of a healthy subject, characterization of the
inlet velocity proﬁle over the mitral valve from ultrasound measurements, along with model
building and simulation in MATLAB® and COMSOL Multiphysics®. Three models are
proposed; a stiﬀmodel, a parametric model, and an ﬂuid-structure interaction (FSI) model.
Simulations are carried out, and all models successfully converge and promising results are
obtained through the study, showing velocities and blood ﬂow patterns in the same range as
found in the literature. The models are thereby a solid foundation for further development
for a potential future 3D model.
v
==PAGE:8==
vi
==PAGE:9==
Resumé
Det menes, at forståelsen af ændringer i venstre ventrikels blodgennemstrømningsmønstre
kan give et bedre indblik i kardiovaskulære sygdomme samt blive brugt klinisk til fore-
byggelse, evaluering og behandling af kardiovaskulære sygdomme. I dette studie udvikles
2D ﬁnite element (FE) modeller til analyse af blodgennemstrømningen i venstre ventrikel
ved at kombinere Computational Fluid Dynamics (CFD), Computer Tomograﬁ(CT) og ul-
tralyd. Modellerne indeholder segmentering af venstre ventrikel fra CT skanninger af en rask
person, beskrivelse af hastighedsproﬁlen over mitral klappen fra ultralydsskanninger, samt
opbygning af modeller og simuleringer i MATLAB® og COMSOL Multiphysics®. Tre mod-
eller er præsenteret; en stiv model, en parametrisk model og en ﬂuid-structure interaction
(FSI) model. Simuleringer udføres, og alle tre modeller konvergerer med succes og lovende
resultater er opnået gennem studiet, der viser hastigheder og mønstre i samme interval som
beskrives i litteraturen. Modellerne er dermed et solidt fundament for videre udvikling til en
potentiel fremtidig 3D model.

CHAPTER 1
Introduction
Cardiovascular diseases (CVDs) are disorders of the heart and the blood vessels, and they are
the primary cause of death globally. More people are dying from these disorders than from any
other cause, and it is estimated that 31 % of all global deaths each year are due to CVDs [1].
It is believed that most CVDs can be prevented with an understanding of the structure and
function of the heart. Thus, great eﬀort is put into examination of the cardiovascular system,
and research shows that mechanical factors have a very close connection to CVDs, being that
changes in the blood ﬂow pattern are seen in most cases [2]. Hence, the understanding of
these alterations in blood ﬂow and dynamics can give a better insight to CVDs, and be used
clinically for prevention, evaluation, and treatment of CVDs.
Information about the blood ﬂow in the heart can be obtained by both invasive and
non-invasive methods. As invasive methods are often diﬃcult and dangerous, they are not
optimal for general research and use. For in vivo measurements, medical imaging methods
such as ultrasound, magnetic resonance imaging (MRI), or computed tomography (CT) are
used. Nonetheless, these methods do not give all details as they give a 2D view of a 3D
phenomena and post-processing might give faulty results. However, it is thought that these
imaging modalities can be used in combination with computational ﬂuid dynamics (CFD)
to give detailed information about the blood ﬂow and thereby a new insight to CVDs. For
the medical imaging applied for this, MRI has a low resolution that limits the evaluation
of geometric details in a ﬁne scale, whereas CT can give details on very small levels, but
uses harmful ionizing radiation.
However, the CT scan radiation dose has been lowered
9
==PAGE:26==
signiﬁcantly over time, whilst maintaining the detailed imaging.
This study develops ﬁnite element (FE) models of the left ventricle of the heart for analysis
of blood ﬂow by combining CFD, CT and ultrasound. The main goal with the development
of these models is to investigate whether it is possible to create an FE model that converges
and gives a realistic solution. Moreover, the work should form a solid foundation for further
work and future development of a 3D model. The 2D models in this study are developed
in steps, where the ventricular geometry is obtained from CT scans, segmentation of the
ventricle is done in Simpleware ScanIP version 7.0, and a further reﬁnement of this in the 3D
CAD software Solidworks 2016. From this, a 2D geometry is created. The 2D models displays
the ﬂuid dynamics in a cross-sectional area of the left ventricle. First, a simple, stiﬀmodel
is made, in which there is no deformation. Hereafter, the model is expanded to a model with
deformation, where the deformation movement during contraction is modelled parametrically,
using clinical standard values for changes in ventricular diameter during the cardiac cycle.
Finally, a deformation model based on ﬂuid-structure interaction (FSI) is proposed, in which
the myocardium surrounding the ventricle is included. The inﬂow velocity for all models is
obtained from ultrasound images. Both the stiﬀmodel and the deformed models are build in
COMSOL Multiphysics® version 5.2a via MATLAB® version 2017a, and the dataprocessing
is carried out in MATLAB® as well. The CT imaging data used in the study are obtained
from the Copenhagen City Heart study [8]. Fig. 1.1 shows the process for the work done
throughout the study.

CHAPTER 2
Clinical Background
Some basic knowledge of the human heart, the cardiac cycle, and blood ﬂow is a prerequisite
to understand the making of an FE model of the heart.
Therefore, this chapter brieﬂy
describes essential anatomy and physiology of the human heart. Moreover, disorders giving
the ventricular malfunctions are described, along with the beneﬁts of a visual presentation
of the inﬂuence they have on the cardiac blood ﬂow.
2.1
Anatomy and physiology of the human heart
The human heart is a vital muscular organ as it pumps blood throughout the body and
thereby delivers oxygen and nutrients to the tissues and removes carbon dioxide as well. It
consists of a right and a left side, each containing an atrium and a ventricle, giving the heart
its four chambers. The tricuspid valve and the mitral valve are the atrioventricular (AV)
valves, connecting the atrium and the ventricle for the right and the left side, respectively.
The pulmonary semi-lunar valve separates the pulmonary artery and right ventricle, whereas
the aortic valve separates the aorta and left ventricle. As the heart contracts and pumps
blood throughout the body it works via two circuits; the pulmonary circuit and the systemic
circuit. In the pulmonary circuit, deoxygenated blood moves through the right side of the
heart, where it leaves the right ventricle by the pulmonary artery and travels to the lungs to
be oxygenated. From here, the oxygenated blood returns from the lungs via the pulmonary
vein to the left atrium of the heart. Here the systemic circuit begins, as the blood continues
13
==PAGE:30==
2.1. ANATOMY AND PHYSIOLOGY OF THE HUMAN HEART
Figure 2.1: The cardiac cycle, showing blood ﬂow through the atrium and ventricles [6].
into the left ventricle, and hereafter leaves the left ventricle, entering the aorta. From the
aorta, the blood enters the arteries and capillaries so it can supply the body’s tissues with the
needed oxygen. The deoxygenated blood afterward returns through the veins and venae cavae
to the right atrium, and so the circulation continues as illustrated in Fig. 2.1. The valves
between the chambers, pulmonary artery, and aorta, ensure that the blood keeps ﬂowing in
one direction through the heart by preventing any backﬂow [3].
The heart contraction is caused by an electrical signal that travels through the cardiac
tissues. It is generated in the sinoatrial node as an action potential, which hereafter excites
the right atrium, and continues through the Bachmann’s bundle for excitation of the left
atrium. From the right atrium the electrical impulse continues to the atrioventricular node
and then further down to the bundle of His. From here, it reaches the Purkinje ﬁbers in the
myocardium that excites the ventricles and hereby make them contract. The contraction of
the heart can be split into multiple phases. The ﬁrst phase is the early diastole where the
heart is relaxed. Afterwards, the atrial systole takes place, and the atrium contracts to push
14 of 118
==PAGE:31==
2.2. DISORDERS AND CLINICAL RELEVANCE
the blood into the ventricle, which is ﬁlled during the so-called ventricular diastole. In the
third phase, the ventricles start contracting, but without changing the ventricular volume.
This is called the isovolumetric contraction. Following this, the ventricular systole occurs
where the ventricle contracts, and pushes the blood out into the aorta or pulmonary artery.
Lastly, there is the isovolumetric relaxation where the ventricle relaxes, but without volume
change. Then the cycle repeats [3][4]. The time of these phases is deﬁned slightly diﬀerently
in the literature. In this study they are set to 0.05 s for isovolumetric contraction, 0.25 s
for systole, 0.05 s for isovolumetric relaxation and 0.45 s for diastole, giving a cardiac cycle
lasting 0.8 s. Besides contraction, the systole also involves a twist of the ventricle. As the
myocardial ﬁbers contract, they simultaneously cause a wringing action which increases the
ejection fraction, i.e. the fraction of blood ejected from the ventricle. With the contraction
alone, the ejection fraction is estimated to be 15 - 20 %, whereas the inclusion of twisting
can increase this fraction to 60 %. The ventricle untwists again in the diastolic phase [5].
During the cycle, the pressure varies in the chambers. In Fig. 2.2 it is shown how the atrial,
ventricular, and aortic pressure change. In the isovolumetric contraction, the ventricular
pressure rises from 0 - 10 mmHg to 80 mmHg. As the semilunar valves open, the ventricular
pressure rises further up to 120 mmHg along with the aortic pressure, and then decreases to
the 100 mmHg again. In the isovolumetric relaxation, the pressure drops back down to the
0 - 10 mmHg. [4]. In this study, the focus lies on the blood ﬂow through the left ventricle;
or more precisely, the entering of blood into the left ventricle via the mitral valve and the
dynamics in the left ventricle.
2.2
Disorders and Clinical relevance
In the above section, the cardiac cycle and blood ﬂow are described. Several changes in
the heart geometry can be imagined to inﬂuence the regular blood ﬂow through the left
ventricle. For example, mitral valve disorders such as stenosis, regurgitation, or artifacts
such as scarring, causing ventricular stiﬀness are easily believed to disturb the blood ﬂow.
In mitral stenosis, the mitral valve opening is narrowed and therefore inhibits the inﬂow of
blood to the ventricle from the atrium. This leads to a decreased amount of oxygenated
15 of 118
==PAGE:32==
2.2. DISORDERS AND CLINICAL RELEVANCE
Figure 2.2: The atrial, ventricular, and aortic pressures during the cardiac cycle. The
arrows indicate the contraction phases. Between AV valves closing and semilunar valves
opening is the isovolumetric contraction. Hereafter the systolic phase takes place until the
semilunar valves close again, followed by the isovolumetric relaxation until the opening of
the AV valves. Lasty the diastolic phase occurs [7].
blood from the lungs, giving shortness of breath. It also increases the pressure and volume in
the atrium which leads to ﬂuid accumulating in the lungs. Another example of a disturbance
in the ventricular blood ﬂow is myocardial scarring, where ﬁbrous tissue replaces the healthy
tissue in the ventricle. Such scarring prevents the electrical signals, causing the contraction
of the ventricle, from reaching the given area of the ventricle. The development of patient-
speciﬁc models of the left ventricular blood ﬂow can help understanding the cardiovascular
pathologies, as well as become a valuable tool for diagnostics of CVDs, or for evaluating the
consequences of surgical procedures in the treatment of these and thereby ﬁnding the optimal
treatment plan.
16 of 118
==PAGE:33==
CHAPTER 3
State of the art
3.1
CFD Modelling
Throughout the last years it has been acknowledged that CFD of the left ventricle is a
useful tool for examining the function of the heart. Diﬀerent approaches have been applied
to implement a CFD model, such as geometry based models and ﬂuid-structure interaction
(FSI) models, described in the literature. This chapter gives an insight to the articles studied
for building the model with focus on the geometry through the cardiac cycle.
In the geometry based CFD models the surrounding structure and its interaction with
the ﬂuid, i.e. the blood, is not considered. Thus, it is only the geometrical deformation
that is included, and this is implemented with prescribed moving meshes or boundaries.
These are either obtained from medical imaging, such as ultrasound imaging, CT or MRI,
or by assumption of a geometric shape to ﬁt the ventricular geometry. An example of such
a model can be found in the article by Evans et al. [9] where a parametric model of the
time-dependent geometry of the left ventricle is created. Here, an elliptic partial diﬀerential
equation is solved to get the surface of the ventricle, and the boundary curves for the cross-
section of the ventricle are assumed to be circular. The geometry is ﬁnally combined with
CFD software for analysis of blood ﬂow. Another geometry based approach is performed by
N. R. Saber et. al. [11] where MRI is used. This is to obtain the ventricular geometry at
the cardiac cycle phases, and interpolation is then applied for intermediate times. In Mir-
Hossein Moosavia et. al. [12] MRI is also used to obtain a patient-speciﬁc geometry of the
17
==PAGE:34==
3.1. CFD MODELLING
left ventricle. Furthermore, the motion of the walls is obtained from cine-MR and Arbitrary
Lagrangian-Eulerian (ALE) ﬁnite element method (FEM) is used for the numerical solution.
Lastly, in Lantz et. al. [15] CT is applied to track the wall motion.
In the FSI model, the interaction between the deformed mesh or boundary is included in
the model. In Cheng et. al. [13], a coupling of the ﬂuid and the structure is implemented
by combining the respective equations in every time step of the simulation. The geometry
applied here is an elliptic shape with thin walls surrounding the ventricular volume.
In
studies by Tang et. al.[14] patient-speciﬁc FSI models are created with MRI for volume
changes throughout the cardiac cycle, and the tissue ﬁber orientation and layer structure
are obtained from literature. Diseased hearts are modelled to show clinical relevance with
regards to pulmonary valve replacement and scar tissue trimming.
The above mentioned articles, along with other articles describing the work with CFD
models, are inserted in Table 3.2 to give an overview of the studied articles. In this study,
the approaches used in the diﬀerent articles have been used for inspiration on how to model
the ventricular geometry, the deformation of the geometry, as well as the ﬂuid properties and
governing equations for the CFD model.

CHAPTER 4
Medical Imaging
As the study of developing a CFD model involves a well-deﬁned geometry, as well as proper
boundary conditions, medical imaging is applied. This chapter covers the medical imaging
techniques used in the study.
4.1
Computed Tomography
In this study, a segmentation of the left ventricle was carried out. The details of this process
are described in the next chapter. However, the medical imaging applied for the segmentation
of the left ventricle was CT scans. Therefore, this section will give a short description of the
theory behind CT.
4.1.1
Theoretical description
CT scans are based on a large set of X-ray projections. The projections are taken from
diﬀerent angles covering the entire patient and are then combined to produce cross-sectional
images of the desired area or object. These images are referred to as "slices", and when
combined they give the 3D information needed. The images are, as mentioned, build on
X-ray imaging technique, and so the main components of a CT scanner are an X-ray tube,
the object to be scanned, and a detection system. The X-ray tube emits a given number
of photons per unit of time in a fan-shaped beam, which passes through the object to be
scanned, and the detector system is placed to measure the photons exiting the object [17][18].
21
==PAGE:38==
4.1. COMPUTED TOMOGRAPHY
The emission is done at a high dose, typically at 50 - 100 millisievert, to avoid noisy images
and ensuring a proper reconstruction of the scan. For each point, or image pixel, of the
object being scanned, a linear attenuation coeﬃcient is being calculated from the detector
system measurements as
I = I0exp(−
Z
µ(x, y)ds),
(4.1)
in which I is the intensity of the X-ray beam and µ(x, y) is the linear attenuation co-
eﬃcient at a given position in the object. This attenuation coeﬃcient is an evaluation of
how easily an X-ray beam can penetrate a given material, thereby quantifying how much the
material weakens the beam passing through it. In other words, a CT scan reconstructs and
gives an image of the attenuation of X-rays by using the density of the body. In the image,
each point is blurry, as a result of back projection degrading the contrast and thereby the
boundary accuracy. To overcome this, the method of ﬁltered back projection is used [18].
The ﬁnal attenuation coeﬃcients obtained are transformed into a special dimensionless unit
used for CT scanning, called Hounsﬁeld unit (HU):
HU = 1000µm −µw
µw
,
(4.2)
where µm and µw are the average linear attenuation coeﬃcients for the image voxel and
water at the same spectrum of photon energies, respectively. So the Hounsﬁeld unit varies
depending on the material, thereby making it possible to identify the diﬀerent types of tissue
in the CT image, which is used during segmentation of the left ventricle [17][18]. Fig. 4.1
shows the setup principles of a CT scan.
22 of 118
==PAGE:39==
4.2. ULTRASOUND
Figure 4.1: A CT scan consists of an X-ray beam, an object (patient) to be scanned, and a
detector system [19].
4.1.2
Data acquisition
The images being used in this study are obtained from the Copenhagen City Study [8].
The information about a scan sequence used for segmentation is shown in table 4.1. Here
the speciﬁc setup for the CT scan, the name of the sequence, and image size can be seen.
Information about the person is removed due to protection of personal information.
4.2
Ultrasound
Besides the imaging modality of CT for segmentation, ultrasound imaging was used to obtain
the blood velocity inlet condition needed for modeling of the blood ﬂow.
4.2.1
Theoretical description
Ultrasound imaging is based on sound waves with a frequency above the audible range, the
so-called ultrasound waves, typically between 1 MHz and 20 MHz for clinical application. For

Figure 4.2: Example of CT image
imaging, these waves are generated by a transducer, they then travel through the diﬀerent
tissues of the object being scanned, and return to the transducer as echoes. The sound beam
is generated in the transducer by the piezoelectric eﬀect, in which electrical pulses creates
mechanical oscillations of crystals in the transducer, thereby creating the sound waves. As
the waves propagate through the object, they are reﬂected and scattered on the surface of
tissues with diﬀerent densities and speeds of sound. This phenomenon is proportional to the
impedance, so if the density change increases, so does the proportion of reﬂected sound. The
24 of 118
==PAGE:41==
4.2. ULTRASOUND
echoes are returned to the transducer where they are converted back into electrical impulses
to be processed further and generate the image [20].
The ultrasound wave propagation is determined by its pressure impact with the tissue
particles. As the waves reach the particles in the tissue, the particles oscillate around their
resting position with a frequency, f depending on the propagation velocity, c, of the waves
and the wavelength, λ:
λf = c.
(4.3)
The waves exerted from the transducer, travel as longitudinal waves in soft tissues and
the propagation speed is calculated as:
c =
r κ
ρ0
.
(4.4)
Here, κ is the bulk modulus and ρ0 is the mass density of the tissue. Hence, the propaga-
tion is related to the density and elastic properties of the tissue. The speed of sound in blood
is approximately 1540 m/s. The following 1D equation for spherical waves can describe the
acoustic pressure that the sound waves exert on the tissue particles:
p(r, t) = P0exp

−j 2πf0t −2πr
λ

,
(4.5)
with r being the distance from the centre of the coordinate system, i.e. the source of the
pressure waves, P0 the pressure magnitude, t is the time, and f0 is the frequency.
Sound waves’ interaction with the tissue is described by the terms reﬂection, refrac-
tion, absorption, scattering, and attenuation. Reﬂection and refraction take place when the
wave reaches an inﬁnite plane between two media with diﬀerent acoustic properties, whereas
scattering occurs when the wave strikes a plane between diﬀerent acoustic properties and a
wavelength smaller than the ultrasounds wavelength. Absorption is the conversion of acoustic
25 of 118
==PAGE:42==
4.2. ULTRASOUND
energy into heat, and ﬁnally, the attenuation is the loss in intensity of the forward propagat-
ing wave and is due to reﬂection, refraction, scattering, and absorption, and can be written
as:
I(x) = I(0)e−αx,
(4.6)
where I(0) is the pressure wave intensity at a reference point, I(x) is the intensity at
point x, and α is the attenuation coeﬃcient, depending on the type of tissue [20].
4.2.2
Data acquisition
The ultrasound data used in this study are obtained with a BK3000 scanner at the Center
for Fast Ultrasound at DTU, where a Ph.D. student volunteered. Both B-mode images and
the spectrogram for the area of interest are included in the scanning and displayed as in
Fig. 4.3a. The transducer used is the convex array BK9040 (6C2) with a center frequency of
3.1 MHz and a pitch of 0.330 mm.
The B-mode images can be seen at the top of the screen, where the data is displayed.
The B stands for brightness and the images are obtained by moving the scan line to diﬀerent
locations next to each other. The transducer is moved across the medium to be scanned
and hereby collects enough image lines to create a whole image of the area of interest. It is
important that the registration period, in which the echo from the emitted pulse is registered,
is long enough so that the echoes from the diﬀerent scan lines are not mixed. A B-mode
image from the measurement used in this study can be seen in Fig. 4.3a.
The spectrogram for the variation in the blood ﬂow velocity over time is shown underneath
the B-mode images in Fig. 4.3a. It displays the velocity that the range gate covers. This
range is marked by the white lines in the image, and the mitral valve is seen just next to
it. The spectral data is also referred to as Doppler data, since it builds on the Doppler
eﬀect, describing the shift in frequency of the ultrasonic waves. This shift in frequency is
due to the motion of red blood cells, being the insoniﬁed scatters. Earlier, the continuous
wave system was used for estimating the blood velocity via the Doppler eﬀect. However, this
26 of 118
==PAGE:43==
4.2. ULTRASOUND
(a) The screen display during an ultrasound
scanning at the mitral valve
(b) The BK3000 ultrasound scanner
Figure 4.3: BK3000 ultrasound screen display and scanner
system has a ﬂaw as it cannot detect the depth in the measured tissue. Therefore, it has a
risk of giving a wrong frequency estimation because two adjacent vessels at diﬀerent depths
might be insoniﬁed at the same time. To estimate blood velocity, the pulsed wave system is
therefore now preferred since it can diﬀerentiate tissue depths. In the pulsed wave system,
short series of ultrasound pulses are emitted and the back-scattered signal is sampled at the
same time intervals. Hence, this method detects the shift in the scatter position and not the
shift in frequency. As it can be seen in Fig. 4.4, there are two waves in the spectre. These are
called the E- and A-wave, respectively. The E-wave shows the initial rush of blood into the
ventricle as the mitral valve opens. Following this, a short period of no ﬂow or very low ﬂow
occurs. Then the atrium contracts and forces a new rush of blood into the ventricle, depicted
by the A-wave. The time from the E peak-velocity to baseline is called the deceleration time
(DT). Normally, it is the raw data ﬁles that are processed to obtain a velocity proﬁle, but
as this is not possible for the scanner used here, an outline of the spectrogram is used. This
method is carried out in MATLAB. In this process, the video from the scan is imported into
MATLAB. Afterwards, a color-threshold is used to isolate the spectrogram, and the baseline
is set to zero. For the time, the DT from the literature [28][29] is used as a reference, hereby
adjusting the time axis. The resulting peak velocities for the A- and E-wave, as well as
27 of 118
==PAGE:44==
4.2. ULTRASOUND
Figure 4.4: The E- and A-wave along with DT from an ultrasound scan [28].
the resulting systolic time, are compared to literature values to ensure that the results are
realistic. The time/velocity matrix is then saved as a text ﬁle so that it can be imported into
COMSOL for the simulation. In Fig. 4.5 the velocity for one cycle is shown. This resulting
velocity matches the literature very well looking at the recommendations by [29].
Figure 4.5: The inlet velocity measured at the mitral valve.
28 of 118
==PAGE:45==
CHAPTER 5
Modelling of the human heart
Modelling of the blood ﬂow and dynamics of the left ventricle includes both a valid geometry
of the ventricle as well as mathematics correctly describing the physical phenomenas taking
place. This chapter therefore covers the geometry and blood ﬂow description used in the
CFD model.
5.1
Geometry
To model the blood ﬂow in the left ventricle and resemble the in vivo situations occurring, an
anatomically correct geometry of the left ventricle must be obtained. This is done by using
CT scans of a healthy human heart for segmentation of the ventricle. The segmentation
is carried out by using the semiautomatic segmentation software, ScanIP, from Simpleware,
and hereafter further processing in the 3D CAD software Solidworks. An important factor
when segmenting the ventricle is that the mitral valve and aortic valve are modelled as open
or closed holes in this study, and these "pseudovalves" open and close instantaneously in the
following simulation.
5.1.1
ScanIP
The DICOM images from the CT scans are loaded into ScanIP for segmentation. Before
beginning the segmentation, the images are cropped and the window size is adjusted. The
geometry of the ventricle is deﬁned as the ventricular volume with cut-oﬀat the mitral- and
29
==PAGE:46==
5.1. GEOMETRY
aortic valve. The segmentation of this in ScanIP is semi-automatic, with a procedure that
can be performed stepwise. Firstly, a rough segmentation is performed by using the threshold
tool where the CT image is segmented based on the its Hounsﬁeld units. The proﬁle line
application of this tool is applied as seen in Fig. 5.3, in which a line across the image is drawn
and the upper- and lower limit are adjusted for the optimal values.
Hereafter the ﬂoodﬁll application is applied. This ﬁlls out the gaps in the volume seg-
mented out in the ﬁrst step, by clicking on a pixel belonging to the ventricle. For optimisation
of the current segmentation, the paint tool is manually used to connect or disconnect pixels
that have been wrongly segmented previously. The paintbrush is varied in size and shape to
get a precise result. The paint tool is also used for removing connections leading to incorrect
areas being added to the segmentation, followed by the ﬂoodﬁll application being applied
again. It is also with the paint tool that the cut oﬀat the mitral valve and aortic valve is
inserted. These are carefully made, ensuring that the painted cut-oﬀfollows the valves. Fi-
nally, the segmented volume must be smoothed. This smoothing removes sharp edges, since
these would inﬂuence the model later on and give errors in the simulation results. For the
smoothing in this study, the recursive Gaussian ﬁlter is applied, corresponding to a noise-
reducing low-pass ﬁltering of the image. The ﬁnal segmented left ventricle is exported as a
cloud-point ﬁle.
5.1.2
Solidworks
When the segmentation in ScanIP is ﬁnished, the geometry is imported into Solidworks. Here,
the geometry is improved for FE modelling. The application mesh prep wizard is applied for
setting the parameters for global, local, and boundary smoothness. Afterwards the surface
wizard is opened, and the guided creation is chosen. Here the surface details are edited, and
regions giving an inverted mesh are knitted manually to eliminate self intersecting surfaces.
Finally, the areas thought to be used for inlet and outlet, being the mitral valve and aortic
valve, are made completely smooth by applying workplanes and extruded cut, where the sizes
are obtained from literature and the segmentation in ScanIP. For the ﬁrst part of the study, a
2D model is desired. Therefore, a cross-sectional area is deﬁned. This is done by inserting a
30 of 118
==PAGE:47==
5.1. GEOMETRY
workplane cutting through the middle of the ventricle as shown in Fig. 5.6b, and the surface
of the cut plane is marked and exported as a DXF-ﬁle to be used for modelling in COMSOL
Multiphysics®. For the FSI model, a wall is added to the geometry by using the oﬀset entity
option in Solidworks. A myocardial wall thickness of 6 mm is implementing according to the
studies by [26] concerning normal left ventricular myocardial thickness.
A ﬂow-chart (Fig. 5.1) with corresponding images for the segmentation process, have been
made to give a step by step description.

5.2. MATHEMATICAL MODELLING
5.2
Mathematical modelling
5.2.1
Blood ﬂow, pressure and material properties
The normal condition for blood throughout the majority of the circulatory system is laminar
ﬂow. Thus, the model in this study is build on laminar ﬂow. In such ﬂow, the blood moves
in parallel layers in the vessels with the highest velocity in the center and the lowest velocity
along the walls. This reduces the energy loss in the blood ﬂow since there is a minimum
viscous interaction between the blood layers and the walls. If the velocity gets very high,
or if arteries are narrowed or diseased, the laminar ﬂow is disturbed and includes turbulent
features such as vortices. Reynolds number describes ﬂow patterns, and laminar ﬂow is when
the value is under 2000. Between 2000 and 4000 the ﬂow is said to be mixed laminar and
turbulent, and if the value exceeds 4000 the ﬂow is fully turbulent.
Re = ρuL
µ ,
(5.1)
with ρ being the density, u the mean velocity, L the linear dimension, and µ the viscosity.
This equation can thereby be used to examine the degree of turbulence, if any, in the blood
ﬂow. The blood is an incompressible ﬂuid with a density of 1060 kg/m3 and viscosity of
0.003 Pa · s, and is modelled accordingly in this study. Moreover, the blood is assumed to
be Newtonian. The shear stress is linear proportional to the viscosity for a Newtonian ﬂuid,
and the viscosity is constant at any shear rate. Additionally, a ﬂuid is deﬁned as Newtonian
if the shear rate is over 100 s−1. Navier-Stokes equation describes the ﬂuid motion. The
equation arises from Newtons second law applied to the motion of ﬂuids, and describes the
conservation of momentum. For an incompressible ﬂuid, the equation takes the following
form:
ρdu
dt + ρ(u · ∇)u = ∇p + µ∇2u + ρ−→g ,
(5.2)
36 of 118
==PAGE:53==
5.2. MATHEMATICAL MODELLING
and it is always solved together with the continuity equation, describing the conservation
of mass:
ρ∇· (u) = 0.
(5.3)
In these equations ρ is the density, u the ﬂow velocity vector, ∇the gradient operator,
p the pressure, and g the gravitational ﬁeld.During the systole, the pressure at the open
valve is set to match the pressure shown in Fig. 2.2. For the isovolumetric contraction and
isovolumetric relaxation, the pressure is set for all boundaries as the valves are now a part of
the wall.
For the FSI model, in which the interaction between the blood and the cardiac wall
is included, the principles of linear elasticity must be introduced. In linear elasticity, the
deformation of a solid object is mathematically described along with the internally stress of
the solid, generated by prescribed loading conditions. The deformation is deﬁned as elastic if
the solid returns to its original shape after the applied load is removed again, and if the ratio
between the deformation and the load is constant, the curve showing stress-strain relationship
is linear. So as to describe this deformation, the elasticity module, Young’s modulus, must
be deﬁned for the myocardium. Young’s modulus is a mechanical property of a linear elastic
solid material, and it is an assessment of the materials stiﬀness [21]. For the myocardium, the
modulus is set to 4 MPa in this study, taken from [22] by A. Hassaballah et. al., where the
tissue compressibility in the left ventricle is determined. In [22] the modulus vary through
the cardiac cycle, whereas it is set to a single value in this study. In relation to the elasticity
modulus and applied load, Poisson’s ratio also have to be determined, as it describes the
ratio of the transverse strain normal to the applied load to the axial strain in the direction
of the applied load [21]. In this study’s FSI model it is set to 0.49, also according to [22].
Finally, the density of the wall must also be deﬁned, and is set to 1200 kg/m3.
37 of 118
==PAGE:54==
5.2. MATHEMATICAL MODELLING
5.2.2
Parametric deformation model
For the 2D model deformation, a parametric approach is applied. In the literature it can
be read how the diameter of the left ventricle changes when the heart is contracting during
systole. Clinically, the terms end-diastolic diameter (EDD), end-systolic diameter (ESD),
and fraction shortening (FS) are used, and connected by the formula [24]:
%FS = EDD −ESD
EDD
100%
(5.4)
The normal shortening for a healthy heart is 25 - 45 % [23]. The equation above can also
be written in a volumetric form, where the end-diastolic volume (EDV), end-systolic volume
(ESV) and ejection fraction (EF) are used:
EF = EDV −ESV
EDV
100(%),
(5.5)
in which EF expresses the ratio of the stroke volume (SV) of the left ventricle to the left
ventricular end-diastolic volume (EDV). Fig. 5.8 shows the short-axis diameter in the left
ventricle at diastole and systole. As it is seen in the Fig.. 5.8 and as it can also be read in
literature, the systolic contraction mainly happens in the short-axis, and the contraction in
the long-axis is very small in comparison.
When implementing the deformation, the formulas developed by Evans et. al. [9][25] are
used for inspiration for continuously changing the radii of the ventricle. Here the deformation
is set to be 30 % for the short-axial radii and 7 % for the long-axial radii, giving the following
prescribed deformation and wall movement:

5.2.3
Fluid-Structure Interaction (FSI) model
When having to address the deformation of the ventricle during the cardiac cycle, a more
accurate result is hoped to be obtained by including the walls surrounding the ventricle along
with their physical properties. This is in order to approach the model as a bidirectionally
analysis, meaning that the ﬂuid ﬂow and the following pressure changes aﬀects the struc-
tural deformation, and that the structural deformation aﬀect the ﬂuid ﬂow and pressure.
Therefore, an FSI model is applied for this part of the study. An FSI model is a model that
couples the laws describing ﬂuid dynamics and the laws describing structural mechanics, at
the boundaries between the solids and the ﬂuid. Such a model can therefore describe the in-
teraction of a deformable or moving structure with an internal ﬂuid, and thus it can describe
the ventricle deformation and blood ﬂow.
39 of 118
==PAGE:56==
5.3. COMSOL MULTIPHYSICS® AND MATLAB®
As the blood in the ventricle encounters the surrounding walls during the diastole, stresses
and strain are exerted on these walls, which leads to a deformation. This deformation is
modelled by using an arbitrary Lagrangian-Eulerian (ALE) method, in which the Eulerian
description of the ﬂuid along with the Lagrangian description of the solids are combined.
COMSOL oﬀers diﬀerent methods for approaching an FSI problem. Choosing the ap-
propriate combination of features is essential for obtaining a well-deﬁned model. One of
the selections, is the choice of solver conﬁguration, where COMSOL oﬀers the fully coupled
method or the segregated method. The segregated approach divides the physics involved in
the problem, and thereby treats each of them sequentially, using the previously calculated
results to compute the next physics loads and material properties. The fully coupled ap-
proach, on the other hand, considers all the physics involved in the problem at the same
time. Therefore, this fully coupled method is applied throughout the ventricular FSI model.
In order to apply the FSI method in COMSOL, a boundary load must be applied to the wall
surrounding the ventricular lumen. This boundary load is diﬃcult to apply, since it is not
known how great of a force is needed to give the correct contraction. It is not enough to
implement the known ventricular pressure that is used within the ventricle, as this does not
include the force generated by the electrical impulses.
5.3
COMSOL Multiphysics® and MATLAB®
The simulation of left ventricular blood ﬂow is made in COMSOL. Here, the geometry from
ScanIP/Solidworks is imported and the descriptive mathematics is deﬁned. This includes
the material properties, parameters, and variables needed. These are shown in Fig. 5.12.
The physics applied is laminar ﬂow for both the stiﬀand deformable model, and deformed
geometry is added in the deformable model. In COMSOL, there are two options for deﬁning
a deformation of the FE mesh. In the 2D model, the deformed geometry is used as oppose
to moving mesh. This is because the deformed geometry is most applicable for ﬂuid domain
modelling with changes in the domain boundary. When changing the boundary shape with
this option, it is implied that mass is being added or removed, whereas the use of the mov-
ing mesh option implies that the mass always stays the same. Laplace is set as the mesh
40 of 118
==PAGE:57==
5.3. COMSOL MULTIPHYSICS® AND MATLAB®
smoothing parameter, and the geometry shape order is set as ﬁrst order. The equations for
the deformation and for the moving walls are implemented via parameters and variables, and
the general extrusion and maximum is applied for ﬁnding the long- and short-axis radii by
extruding the boundary applicable for deﬁning the radii. The laminar ﬂow is used since this
applies the physical and material properties described in section 5.2.1, and the Navier-Stokes
equations governing the ﬂuid motion are here set to computations for incompressible ﬂuid.
Reynolds number is calculated to investigate whether the blood ﬂow leans toward a turbu-
lent behaviour. When having to model the deformation described in the previous section,
the wall properties with respect to the blood ﬂow must be prescribed. In this study the
walls are set as moving wall, meaning that the ﬂuid will have a velocity equal to the walls.
The mesh for the computations is set to triangular with normal or ﬁne size, depending on
the model in question, as this gives more precise results. Meshing of models in COMSOL
is a tricky matter. You have to deﬁne a regular mesh with the nodes distributed in a way,
so that the length of the edges are even, and that the displacements during the simulation
are distributed as equally as possible between all the sub-items in the mesh, as this gives
the best representation of the changes in the model. If an unsuitable mesh is implemented
in a model with rather large deformations, as in this model, the mesh becomes distorted or
inverted, causing disrupted results. So a higher number of nodes gives a better result, but it
also takes a lot longer to run the simulations. Thus, a balance must be found when meshing.
For the stiﬀmodels, a normal size mesh was applied, which gave a complete mesh consisting
of 12230 elements, with a minimum quality of 0.6415 and an average quality of 0.9549 in
quality scale from 0 to 1. For the deformable models, a ﬁne size mesh was applied, which
gave a complete mesh consisting of 1963 elements, with a minimum quality of 0.2568 and an
average quality of 0.8249. Lastly, the automatic re-meshing function is enabled in the para-
metric model including deformation in order to obtain the desired deformation with regards
to deformation extent and geometry. As explained earlier, the pseudo valves are modelled
as open boundaries. Therefore, these boundaries are switched between being an inlet/outlet
and being a part of the ventricular wall, depending on the phases and whether or not the
valves are open.
The ﬂow velocity through the simulation of the cardiac cycle is depicted as a surface plot.
41 of 118
==PAGE:58==
5.3. COMSOL MULTIPHYSICS® AND MATLAB®
Moreover, the pressure is also set to be plotted to visualise its distribution in the diﬀerent
phases. To give the possibility of controlling the model by scripting, the COMSOL model
for the stiﬀmodel and for the parametric model is build in MATLAB. The main purpose
of the MATLAB scripting is looping over the four phases included in the model, i.e. the
systolic, diastolic and the two isovolumetric phases. The looping is made in order to remove
the inﬂuence of the stationary scenario that initiates the study. For every phase, the initial
values are the resulting values from the previous phase, hereby creating a transient study.
This system is repeated multiple times via the MATLAB loop, thereby creating a ﬁnal, valid
result. Moreover, the pressure throughout the cycle, the inﬂow velocity, as well as the moving
wall and deformation through a cycle are calculated in MATLAB. These are then imported
into the looping script when running.
The scripts giving the resulting COMSOL settings are found in appendix C and D, and
the resulting features and component couplings are shown in appendix A. Parameters and
variables, as well as a screenshot of an example from the COMSOL model builder are seen
in Fig. 5.12:

==PAGE:59==
5.3. COMSOL MULTIPHYSICS® AND MATLAB®
Figure 5.12: COMSOL layout

CHAPTER 6
Results
The following chapter presents the results obtained with the models developed throughout
this study. The results are presented through selected plots at diﬀerent times of the cardiac
cycle, and it is brieﬂy described what can be seen in the various plots. How the given results
correlate to other studies revolving FE modeling of the human heart, and what is seen in
medical imaging, is discussed in chapter seven. The following results are divided into the
models, from where they are obtained. In the stiﬀmodel having a Moving Wall feature, the
velocity along the walls is set to a given velocity. In this study, the velocity in the Moving
Wall feature is set to the velocity of the wall movement. After the plots, a scheme showing
the peak velocity, average velocity, average pressure, as well as Reynolds number over the
inlet/mitral valve and outlet/aortic valve, is presented.

6.1. STIFF MODEL WITH MOVING WALL
In this model, it is seen that the blood ﬂow is directed mostly towards the outlet in the
beginning of the diastole and only slightly towards the rest of the ventricle with a velocity
range of approximately 0.6 m/s. As the diastole continues, the velocity increases and has a
wider direction range and also a wider velocity range. Hereafter, vortices are developed at the
base of the ventricle, beneath the mitral valve, with a decreased velocity. In the isovolumetric
contraction, the velocity decreases further. During the systole, the vortices slowly break up
and the ﬂow is directed out of the ventricle through the aortic valve. In the pressure plots, it
is seen how the beginning of the diastole has a distribution in pressure where there is a lower
pressure at the aortic valve than in the rest of the ventricle. The diﬀerence in pressure here is
close to 4 mmHg. As the diastole continues, the pressure diﬀerence rises a bit. Thereafter it
falls to only 1 - 1.5 mmHg, developing two circular forms having a low pressure in the middle
and higher pressures around. During the isovolumetric contraction, the pressure rises again,
and during systole rises even more and forms a contour centering toward the outlet.

6.2. PARAMETRIC MODEL
In this parametric model, the diastole also begins with the blood ﬂow heading towards
the outlet, and hereafter expands, and develops vortices at the base of the ventricle. The
velocity covers a range of approximately 1 m/s throughout the diastole. The isovolumetric
contraction and the systole starts out with a larger vortex developing in the middle of the
ventricle with a decreased velocity, and afterwards the blood ﬂow turns and directs towards
the outlet with an increased velocity. For the parametric model, the pressure distribution
also begins with having a lower pressure at the aortic valve than in the rest of the ventricle.
Here, the diﬀerence in pressure is around 1 - 1.5 mmHg. The pressure rises a bit during
the diastole, and so does the pressure diﬀerence. Then, the pressure falls again, with two
circular forms having a low pressure in the middle and higher pressure around, one of them
being more distinct than the other. In the isovolumetric contraction, the pressure diﬀerence
is minimal. In the beginning of the systole, a greater circle is developed towards the middle
of the ventricle, and as the systole continues the circle vanishes and the contours form more
clearly, centering towards the outlet. In the isovolumetric relaxation, the pressure diﬀerence
is minimal, as in the isovolumetric contraction.

6.3. FSI MODEL
It was experienced that the FSI model could only converge, when reducing the inlet
velocity. The diastole begins with an inﬂow directly into the ventricle, and only a bit towards
the aortic valve. As in the two other models it seen that vortices are developed beneath the
mitral valve. During the systole, the ﬂow very quickly turns and is headed out the aortic
valve. Later in the systole, the ﬂow out of the ventricle stops for a short period, for then again
to turn out in the end of the systole. The turn in the ﬂow during the systole is suspected
to be due to a vacuum-similar eﬀect, as the pressure changes trough the systole. Moreover,
it is generally seen that the ﬂow is very unstable in the FSI model. Especially during the
isovolumetric relaxation, the ﬂow is constantly shifting its direction and pattern. Looking at
the pressure for the FSI model, it is seen that the pressure diﬀerence in the ventricle during
early diastole is around 10 mmHg, and that two pressure circles are developed throughout
the diastole. In the isovolumetric contraction, the pressure seems to decrease. During systole
the pressure rises again. Finally, in the isovolumetric relaxation it drops drastically. It is
also seen that the ventricular deformation is larger than anticipated with a high EDD. The
same goes for the pressure values within the ventricle that also reach unusually high and low
values throughout the cardiac cycle.

CHAPTER 7
Discussion
This chapter discusses the results described in the previous chapter. The observations are
evaluated when compared to results found in articles, the limitations in the study are dis-
cussed, and the chapter is ﬁnally completed with thoughts on future work and expansion of
this study.
7.1
Comparison with other studies
When evaluating the observed blood ﬂow behaviour in the models developed in this study,
the obtained results are compared to results from MRI velocity scans and ultrasound velocity
scans from selected articles.
In this study, diﬀerent maximums and minimums for the velocity in the simulations were
seen. Therefore it is obvious to examine these in contrast to what is found in the literature.
In the studies by Kim et. al. [30] MRI velocity mapping was used for investigating the
velocities in the left ventricle in healthy subjects. Here, it was found that the maximum
velocity was around 0.32 - 0.73 m/s at the mitral inlet and that the peak outlet velocity was
0.6 - 1.65 m/s. These inlet velocity values ﬁts very well with the inlet velocity measured with
ultrasound in this study, and the peak outlet velocities from this study’s stiﬀand parametric
models are also within the range given by [30]. However, with the FSI model, a higher peak
outlet velocity was found. The average outlet results obtained in this study correlate well
with the work by Lantz et. al. [15] when looking at the cross-sectional area results that they
61
==PAGE:78==
7.1. COMPARISON WITH OTHER STUDIES
show from a 3D model. Here, the outlet velocities are around 0.3 m/s during systole and the
inlet velocities around 0.15 m/s during diastole. Moreover, the velocities are very small in the
apex of the ventricle and barely reach the apex during diastole in [15], which is also clearly
seen in this study’s results. Such small velocities towards and at the apex of the ventricle
are also seen in Spühler et. al. [16], where also the mean outﬂow velocities are similar to the
ones in this study, being in the range of 0.3 to 0.35 m/s. Jacobsen et. al. [10] evaluates a
2D model against magnetic resonance velocity mapping, and ﬁnds simulated inlet velocities
peak at 0.75 m/s for the E-wave and at 0.41 m/s for the A-wave, against MRI values of 0.66
m/s and 0.23 m/s. Their MRI outﬂow velocity peaks around 0.8 m/s. Again, the velocities
match the results in this study very nicely, except for the FSI outlet being a bit higher and
the inlet velocity results a bit under. In the studies by Saber et. al. [11], the maximum
diastolic velocity was found a couple of centimeters below the mitral valve with a velocity of
0.52 m/s, and a lower mitral peak inlet velocity of 0.42 m/s. These values do not correlate
as well with what is found in this study, except for the FSI model which could only converge
with reduced velocities. It is also pointed out in this article, that the peak velocities are seen
at the posterior wall, as the blood ﬂow direction is towards this wall. Such an observation
is not possible here, since it is a 2D model. However, Saber et. al. [11] also points out that
two vortices beneath the mitral valve are developed and later dissolved, turning in to a large
circular current, before ﬁnally heading to the outlet, just as seen in this study. Actually,
vortices were developed beneath the mitral valve during diastole in all of the models in this
study. This phenomena is described in the majority of the articles studied. In Kim et. al.
[30], an anterior vortex with a radius of 1.62 ± 0.24 cm was observed, and in the studies in
a 2D model by Jacobsen et. al. [10], the vortex radius was simulated to be 1 - 2.5 cm and
measured by MRI to be 0.5 - 2 cm. When looking at the results in this study, the vortices
seems to correspond to such size range.
The pressure is also interesting to investigate further in relation to the literature. However,
the pressure is rarely described in the articles concerning CFD models of the ventricle. Saber
et. al. [11] points out that peak positive intra ventricular pressure diﬀerence was 9.3 mmHg
during peak systole in the simulation, then falls to -2 mmHg during late systole, and then
rises towards zero and positive values in the diastole. Saber et. al. conﬁrms that these
62 of 118
==PAGE:79==
7.1. COMPARISON WITH OTHER STUDIES
values are consistent with values measured by Pasipoularides et. al. [31], where ventricular
pressure was measured with a catheter in healthy subjects, and the average values were 121
± 20 mmHg for the systole and 9 ± 2 mmHg for the diastole. In these models it is seen
that the pressure is higher at systole, and in table. 6.1 it is seen that the average values
corresponds very well with [31], though having higher standard deviations. This is probably
since Pasipoularides et. al. [31] has looked at standard deviation for end-diastole, whereas
the results in table. 6.1 are from the entire diastole. The diﬀerence in pressure in the ventricle
is generally seen to be 1 - 4 mmHg in the stiﬀand parametric model. For the FSI model, the
pressure diﬀerence within the ventricle has a higher range and a higher standard deviation
than in the other models. Also, the pressure reaches negative values as seen by [11], though
more extreme. The diﬀerences in pressure between the models is probably caused by the
applied stress in the stiﬀand parametric model versus the outer boundary load applied in
the FSI model.
Several of the above mentioned diﬀerences can be related to the segmentation, especially
the valve cut-oﬀs. They were modelled as plane cuts, which of course is a compression of
the true valve shape. This sort of compression might have had an inﬂuence on the ﬂow
pattern and velocities at the valve regions, considering that no leaﬂet movement have been
included. However, it seems that the ﬂow pattern established by previous work, including
the development of vortices beneath the mitral valve, is obtained. The reason for this pattern
must therefore be caused by something other than the leaﬂets, such as the shear between
the resting ﬂuid in the ventricle and the inlet jet. Another possible inﬂuencing factor on the
obtained results, could be that the atrium and aorta are not included in these models, along
with the potential eﬀect they could have on the ventricular simulation. Lastly, it must be
emphasised that the blood ﬂow pattern can vary in the anterior and posterior part of the
ventricle, and since the models in this study are 2D models only containing the cross-sectional
area, such variances are not implemented or visible.
The models should of course also be compared internally. It is here seen that the FSI
model reaches higher outﬂow velocity in the systole than the stiﬀand parametric model,
and lower diastolic velocity. The higher values for the FSI model are also observed when
63 of 118
==PAGE:80==
7.2. LIMITATIONS AND MODEL CONSIDERATIONS
looking at the average diastolic pressure values. It seems like the pressure during systole
rises faster in the parametric model than in the stiﬀmodel, but overall these two models
have similar pressure distributions. A characteristic that is noticed for the parametric model
and the FSI model, is that the velocity changes more clearly reach the apex during systole,
whereas it is partly missing in the stiﬀmodel. It seems that the apex has a smaller inﬂuence
and movement in the stiﬀmodel than in the parametric model and FSI model, although all
models show low velocities. The parametric model and stiﬀmodel are very alike looking
at the velocities, but the parametric model creates a centered vortex including more of the
ventricle during the isovolumetric contraction and systole than the stiﬀmodel do. Looking
at Reynolds number, it seems that the stiﬀmodel and the parametric model gives a mixed
laminar and turbulent ﬂow, whereas the FSI model has a fully turbulent outﬂow. The FSI
model is seen to have a diﬀerent ﬂow pattern than the stiﬀand parametric model. Here the
blood ﬂow is not directed as much towards the outlet in the beginning of the diastole as it
is with the other two models. Furthermore, the FSI model has a peculiar behavior during
the isovolumetric relaxation with the ﬂow constantly shifting its direction, which is not seen
in the stiﬀmodel or the parametric model. The FSI model is obtaining a larger diameter
than expected after the diastole, when comparing to the diameters and expectations from
the other models. However, the deformation is still within the range of normal shortening
fraction, described in chapter ﬁve. As mentioned above, the blood ﬂow in the stiﬀmodel and
in the parametric model is headed more explicit towards the aortic valve in the beginning of
the diastole, than it is in the FSI model. When the valves open and close in the ventricle, it
happens over a very short period of time. As the leaﬂets are not included in this study and
the shift between open and closed state is instantaneous, it is suspected that the pressure is
not redistributed in the ventricle as it would with a smoother transition, before the diastole
begins. This might lead to the strong ﬂow towards the outlet during early diastole.
7.2
Limitations and model considerations
When looking at the obtained models and results, multiple aspects must be considered.
Firstly, only six months were available for this particular study. Had more time been available,
64 of 118
==PAGE:81==
7.3. FUTURE WORK
more ideas for advancing the models could have been implemented and tested. Some of these
ideas are pointed out in section 7.3 concerning future work.
Moreover, the parameters,
variables, and feature values can be discussed. To start with, the inlet velocity is measured
at one heart rate whilst the parametric deformation of the model is at a given pace, and
these two are not certain to be exactly alike. In context to this, the boundary load applied
for the FSI model is also a diﬃcult feature. The boundary load is enforced as pressure giving
a contraction of the ventricle. It can not be ensured that this pressure is correct to use. In
reality, only the pressure inside the ventricular lumen has been measured clinically, and in
addition, the contribution from the electrical signals exciting the ventricles is not known.
Moreover, a wall thickness of 6 mm is uniformly implemented for the FSI model. This is of
course also a simpliﬁcation of the ventricular geometry as the wall thickness and properties
varies depending on the location of the myocardium, and therefore may be the cause of the
more extreme deformation and converging diﬃculties. Lastly, one can discuss the choice of
simulation program. Here, COMSOL was chosen as this is the program practiced at the
Center for Fast Ultrasound Imaging at DTU. However, this choice of program is very rare in
the literature in which many other simulation programs are highlighted. Whether or not this
could have an inﬂuence, due to diﬀerences in programming possibilities and feature selections,
is currently hard to determine.
7.3
Future work
Several expansions or reﬁnements should be added in future work with the CFD model, both
in the 2D model and in a possible 3D model.
It would deﬁnitely be very interesting to expand the model to a more complex 3D model.
The geometry for such a model is the same as for the 2D model before the cross-sectional
cut, i.e. the full volume depicted in Fig. 5.5b and 5.6b. In a 3D model, the aspect of rotation
during the contraction could also be included, thereby giving a more precise result. Making
a model with the third dimension added, could be carried out with diﬀerent approaches.
A kinematic model approach with predeﬁned equations for geometry movements could be
65 of 118
==PAGE:82==
7.3. FUTURE WORK
applied, for example inspired by the work in [9]. Such a model can give an understanding
of the deformation and how it can be simulated. As a further development from here, an
analytically model could be tested, giving the possibility of illustrating the movement of the
speciﬁc scanned heart. For this, speciﬁc points in the heart surface is followed. Certain
points are marked, and CT scans from diﬀerent times of the cardiac cycle are used to acquire
the deformation data. The time between the images used is interpolated to prevent drastic
transitions. In this way, it is possible to obtain the exact radial contraction in the short-
and long-axis as well as the rotational movement. This is then to be transformed into a
mathematical formulation to be implemented in the simulation program, COMSOL, hereby
also allowing to investigate the eﬀect of the twisting in the ventricle. With a third dimension
added, it would additionally be very interesting to see whether or not the inclusion of pap-
illary muscles would aﬀect the simulation results. This was also done by Lantz et. al. [15],
where the muscles were seen blocking a fully developed blood ﬂow towards the apex.
As a start for the future prospects, the analytically approach of tracking the hearts motion
could be applied in the 2D model, before adding a third dimension. Finally, patients with
various heart conditions could be included in both the 2D model and the future 3D model to
visualise the relevance of a CFD model for a better understanding of CVDs and blood ﬂow.
66 of 118
==PAGE:83==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
In conclusion, ﬁnite element models that converge toward reasonable solutions have been
created. A foundation for the simulation of blood ﬂow in the left ventricle has been developed
by combing medical imaging and CFD simulations. A successful segmentation from CT scans
of the left ventricle was carried out, and a valid inlet velocity proﬁle was obtained through
ultrasound.
The results from the models were analyzed and compared to the literature, from where it
was conﬁrmed that results within the range of realistic values were obtained. It was through
the study established, that vortices are created beneath the mitral valve during diastole as a
consequence of the shear between the resting ﬂuid and incoming jet stream. The work done
in the study supports the idea of simulating the blood ﬂow in the left ventricle by combing
CT scanning and CFD in order to obtain a greater knowledge of the complex patterns taking
place in the ventricle. Moreover, it showed that both stiﬀmodels and deformable 2D models
can give an insight to the blood ﬂow pattern, as long as proper boundary conditions are
implemented. The models created are a solid groundwork for further development including
more details, such as the third dimension or inclusion of the atrium and aorta, and ultimately
models may be used for clinical studies.

A. COMSOL PROPERTIES
A
COMSOL properties
The following properties are the same as for the stiﬀmodels, just with added Deformed Ge-
ometry features. The pressure and velocity functions are the same for all models, and so are
the component couplings.

Matlab Code for Stiﬀ2D Model
Only the loop is shown here. Moving walls are enabled/disabled depending on which stiﬀ
model has to run. ML is the number of cycles wanted, and is given as input to the function.
% Looping in order to repeat the cardiac cycle, ensuring that it is stable and unaﬀected by the ﬁrst studies.
sn = 1 ;
f o r
i = 2:ML
i f
i == 2
sn = sn +5;
e l s e
sn = sn +4;
end

Matlab Code for Stiﬀ2D Parametric model
Only the loop is shown here. Moving walls are enabled/disabled depending on which model
has to run. ML is the number of cycles wanted, and is given as input to the function. The
base model including the ﬁrst cycle is created in Comsol, then loaded into the following
script, where the loop adds the wanted number of extra studies.

function
MovieComsol = MakeMovie(Type)
% Type: Enter 1 for velocity video, and enter 2 for pressure video
% MATLAB must be coupled to a comsol server in order to run the script
% Creating movie:
% Deﬁning the model to be used:
model = mphopen( 'SineDTU8 .mph ' ) ;
% Extracting velocity and pressure data for the four phases in the last
% cycle
pd30 = mpheval ( model ,{ ' spf .U' , 'u ' , ' v ' , 'p ' } , ' dataset ' ,
' dset11 ' ) ;
pd31 = mpheval ( model ,{ ' spf .U' , 'u ' , ' v ' , 'p ' } , ' dataset ' ,
' dset7 ' ) ;
pd32 = mpheval ( model ,{ ' spf .U' , 'u ' , ' v ' , 'p ' } , ' dataset ' ,
' dset9 ' ) ;
